<li><a href="/wiki/Mechanosynthesis" title="Mechanosynthesis">Mechanosynthesis</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Molecular_engineering" title="Molecular engineering">Molecular engineering</a></li></ul></td>
</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-below">
<ul><li><span class="nowrap"><span class="noviewer" typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Nuvola_apps_kalzium.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="icon" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Nuvola_apps_kalzium.svg/20px-Nuvola_apps_kalzium.svg.png" decoding="async" width="15" height="15" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Nuvola_apps_kalzium.svg/40px-Nuvola_apps_kalzium.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="128" data-file-height="128" /></a></span> </span><a href="/wiki/Portal:Science" title="Portal:Science">Science&#32;portal</a></li>
<li><span class="nowrap"><span class="skin-invert-image noviewer" typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Noun-technology.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="icon" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Noun-technology.svg/20px-Noun-technology.svg.png" decoding="async" width="15" height="15" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Noun-technology.svg/40px-Noun-technology.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="90" data-file-height="88" /></a></span> </span><a href="/wiki/Portal:Technology" title="Portal:Technology">Technology&#32;portal</a></li></ul></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333133064" /><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1239400231">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}html.skin-theme-clientpref-night .mw-parser-output .navbar li a abbr{color:var(--color-base)!important}@media(prefers-color-scheme:dark){html.skin-theme-clientpref-os .mw-parser-output .navbar li a abbr{color:var(--color-base)!important}}@media print{.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:none!important}}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Nanotechnology" title="Template:Nanotechnology"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Nanotechnology" title="Template talk:Nanotechnology"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a href="/wiki/Special:EditPage/Template:Nanotechnology" title="Special:EditPage/Template:Nanotechnology"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Nanotechnology</b> is the manipulation of matter with at least one dimension sized from 1 to 100 <a href="/wiki/Nanometers" class="mw-redirect" title="Nanometers">nanometers</a> (nm). At this scale, commonly known as the <b>nanoscale</b>, <a href="/wiki/Surface_area" title="Surface area">surface area</a> and <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanical" class="mw-redirect" title="Quantum mechanical">quantum mechanical</a> effects become important in describing properties of matter. This definition of nanotechnology includes all types of research and technologies that deal with these special properties. It is common to see the plural form "nanotechnologies" as well as "nanoscale technologies" to refer to research and applications whose common trait is scale.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Engines1&#95;1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Engines1-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> An earlier understanding of nanotechnology referred to the particular technological goal of precisely manipulating atoms and molecules for fabricating macroscale products, now referred to as <a href="/wiki/Molecular_nanotechnology" title="Molecular nanotechnology">molecular nanotechnology</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Nanotsystems&#95;2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nanotsystems-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Nanotechnology defined by scale includes fields of science such as <a href="/wiki/Surface_science" title="Surface science">surface science</a>, <a href="/wiki/Organic_chemistry" title="Organic chemistry">organic chemistry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Molecular_biology" title="Molecular biology">molecular biology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor_physics" class="mw-redirect" title="Semiconductor physics">semiconductor physics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Energy_storage" title="Energy storage">energy storage</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Engineering" title="Engineering">engineering</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Microfabrication" title="Microfabrication">microfabrication</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Molecular_engineering" title="Molecular engineering">molecular engineering</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The associated research and applications range from extensions of conventional <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor_device" title="Semiconductor device">device physics</a> to <a href="/wiki/Molecular_self-assembly" title="Molecular self-assembly">molecular self-assembly</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> from developing <a href="/wiki/Nanomaterial" class="mw-redirect" title="Nanomaterial">new materials</a> with dimensions on the nanoscale to <a href="/wiki/Molecular_nanotechnology" title="Molecular nanotechnology">direct control of matter on the atomic scale</a>.
</p><p>Nanotechnology may be able to create new materials and devices with diverse <a href="/wiki/List_of_nanotechnology_applications" class="mw-redirect" title="List of nanotechnology applications">applications</a>, such as in <a href="/wiki/Nanomedicine" title="Nanomedicine">nanomedicine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nanoelectronics" title="Nanoelectronics">nanoelectronics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nanotechnology_in_agriculture" title="Nanotechnology in agriculture">agricultural sectors</a>,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2025)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> <a href="/wiki/Biomaterial" title="Biomaterial">biomaterials</a> energy production, and consumer products. However, nanotechnology raises issues, including concerns about the <a href="/wiki/Nanotoxicology" title="Nanotoxicology">toxicity</a> and environmental impact of nanomaterials,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and their potential effects on global economics, as well as various <a href="/wiki/Grey_goo" class="mw-redirect" title="Grey goo">doomsday scenarios</a>. These concerns have led to a debate among advocacy groups and governments on whether special <a href="/wiki/Regulation_of_nanotechnology" title="Regulation of nanotechnology">regulation of nanotechnology</a> is warranted.
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Origins">Origins</h2></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_nanotechnology" title="History of nanotechnology">History of nanotechnology</a></div>
<p>The concepts that seeded nanotechnology were first discussed in 1959 by physicist <a href="/wiki/Richard_Feynman" title="Richard Feynman">Richard Feynman</a> in his talk <i><a href="/wiki/There%27s_Plenty_of_Room_at_the_Bottom" title="There&#39;s Plenty of Room at the Bottom">There's Plenty of Room at the Bottom</a></i>, in which he described the possibility of synthesis via direct manipulation of atoms.
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Comparison_of_nanomaterials_sizes.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Comparison_of_nanomaterials_sizes.jpg/500px-Comparison_of_nanomaterials_sizes.jpg" decoding="async" width="450" height="415" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Comparison_of_nanomaterials_sizes.jpg/960px-Comparison_of_nanomaterials_sizes.jpg 2x" data-file-width="7824" data-file-height="7216" /></a><figcaption>Comparison of nanomaterials sizes</figcaption></figure>
<p>The term "nano-technology" was first used by <a href="/wiki/Norio_Taniguchi" title="Norio Taniguchi">Norio Taniguchi</a> in 1974, though it was not widely known. Inspired by Feynman's concepts, <a href="/wiki/K._Eric_Drexler" title="K. Eric Drexler">K. Eric Drexler</a> used the term "nanotechnology" in his 1986 book <i><a href="/wiki/Engines_of_creation" class="mw-redirect" title="Engines of creation">Engines of Creation: The Coming Era of Nanotechnology</a></i>, which achieved popular success and helped thrust nanotechnology into the public sphere.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In it, he proposed the idea of a nanoscale "assembler" that would be able to build a copy of itself and of other items of arbitrary complexity with atom-level control. Also in 1986, Drexler co-founded <a href="/wiki/The_Foresight_Institute" class="mw-redirect" title="The Foresight Institute">The Foresight Institute</a> to increase public awareness and understanding of nanotechnology concepts and implications.
</p><p>The emergence of nanotechnology as a field in the 1980s occurred through the convergence of Drexler's theoretical and public work, which developed and popularized a conceptual framework, and experimental advances that drew additional attention to the prospects<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2025)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>. In the 1980s, two breakthroughs helped to spark the growth of nanotechnology. First, the invention of the <a href="/wiki/Scanning_tunneling_microscope" title="Scanning tunneling microscope">scanning tunneling microscope</a> in 1981 enabled visualization of individual atoms and bonds, and was successfully used to manipulate individual atoms in 1989. The microscope's developers <a href="/wiki/Gerd_Binnig" title="Gerd Binnig">Gerd Binnig</a> and <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Rohrer" title="Heinrich Rohrer">Heinrich Rohrer</a> at <a href="/wiki/IBM_Zurich_Research_Laboratory" class="mw-redirect" title="IBM Zurich Research Laboratory">IBM Zurich Research Laboratory</a> received a <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Physics" title="Nobel Prize in Physics">Nobel Prize in Physics</a> in 1986.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Binnig&#95;11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Binnig-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Binnig, <a href="/wiki/Calvin_F._Quate" class="mw-redirect" title="Calvin F. Quate">Quate</a> and Gerber also invented the analogous <a href="/wiki/Atomic_force_microscopy" title="Atomic force microscopy">atomic force microscope</a> that year.
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:C60_Molecule.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/C60_Molecule.svg/250px-C60_Molecule.svg.png" decoding="async" width="200" height="197" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/C60_Molecule.svg/500px-C60_Molecule.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="576" data-file-height="566" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Buckminsterfullerene" title="Buckminsterfullerene">Buckminsterfullerene</a> C<sub>60</sub>, also known as the <a href="/wiki/Buckyball" class="mw-redirect" title="Buckyball">buckyball</a>, is a representative member of the <a href="/wiki/Allotropes_of_carbon" title="Allotropes of carbon">carbon structures</a> known as <a href="/wiki/Fullerene" title="Fullerene">fullerenes</a>. Members of the fullerene family are a major subject of research falling under the nanotechnology umbrella.</figcaption></figure>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1273380762/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tmulti .text-align-left{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-right{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-center{text-align:center}@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:none!important;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle .thumbcaption{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow>.thumbcaption{text-align:center}}@media screen{html.skin-theme-clientpref-night .mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner .multiimageinner span:not(.skin-invert-image):not(.skin-invert):not(.bg-transparent) img{background-color:white}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:452px;max-width:452px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:152px;max-width:152px"><div class="thumbimage"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Harold_Kroto_2007.jpg" /></a></span></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:177px;max-width:177px"><div class="thumbimage"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Iijima.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/aa/Iijima.jpg" decoding="async" width="175" height="175" class="mw-file-element" data-file-width="160" data-file-height="160" /></a></span></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:117px;max-width:117px"><div class="thumbimage"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Richard_Smalley.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/bf/Richard_Smalley.png" decoding="async" width="115" height="232" class="mw-file-element" data-file-width="99" data-file-height="200" /></a></span></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">Harry Kroto (left) won the 1996 Nobel Prize in Chemistry along with Richard Smalley (right) and Robert Curl for their 1985 discovery of <a href="/wiki/Buckminsterfullerene" title="Buckminsterfullerene">buckminsterfullerene</a>, while Sumio Iijima (middle) won the inaugural 2008 <a href="/wiki/Kavli_Prize" title="Kavli Prize">Kavli Prize</a> in Nanoscience for his 1991 discovery of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_nanotubes" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbon nanotubes">carbon nanotubes</a>.</div></div></div></div>
<p>Second, <a href="/wiki/Fullerenes" class="mw-redirect" title="Fullerenes">fullerenes</a> (buckyballs) were discovered in 1985 by <a href="/wiki/Harry_Kroto" title="Harry Kroto">Harry Kroto</a>, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Smalley" title="Richard Smalley">Richard Smalley</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Curl" title="Robert Curl">Robert Curl</a>, who together won the 1996 <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Chemistry" title="Nobel Prize in Chemistry">Nobel Prize in Chemistry</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> C<sub>60</sub> was not initially described as nanotechnology; the term was used regarding subsequent work with related <a href="/wiki/Carbon_nanotube" title="Carbon nanotube">carbon nanotubes</a> (sometimes called <a href="/wiki/Graphene" title="Graphene">graphene</a> tubes or Bucky tubes) which suggested potential applications for nanoscale electronics and devices. The discovery of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_nanotubes" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbon nanotubes">carbon nanotubes</a> is attributed to <a href="/wiki/Sumio_Iijima" title="Sumio Iijima">Sumio Iijima</a> of <a href="/wiki/NEC" title="NEC">NEC</a> in 1991,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-carbon&#95;15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-carbon-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> for which Iijima won the inaugural 2008 <a href="/wiki/Kavli_Prize" title="Kavli Prize">Kavli Prize</a> in Nanoscience.
</p><p>In the early 2000s, the field garnered increased scientific, political, and commercial attention that led to both controversy and progress. Controversies emerged regarding the definitions and potential implications of nanotechnologies, exemplified by the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Royal Society</a>'s report on nanotechnology.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-royalsociety&#95;16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-royalsociety-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Challenges were raised regarding the feasibility of applications envisioned by advocates of molecular nanotechnology, which culminated in a public debate between Drexler and Smalley in 2001 and 2003.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-counterpoint&#95;17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-counterpoint-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Meanwhile, commercial products based on advancements in nanoscale technologies began emerging. These products were limited to bulk applications of <a href="/wiki/Nanomaterials" title="Nanomaterials">nanomaterials</a> and did not involve atomic control of matter. Some examples include the <a href="/wiki/Silver_Nano" title="Silver Nano">Silver Nano</a> platform for using <a href="/wiki/Silver_nanoparticles" class="mw-redirect" title="Silver nanoparticles">silver nanoparticles</a> as an <a href="/wiki/Bactericide" title="Bactericide">antibacterial agent</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nanoparticle" title="Nanoparticle">nanoparticle</a>-based sunscreens, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_fibers" title="Carbon fibers">carbon fiber</a> strengthening using <a href="/wiki/Silicon_dioxide" title="Silicon dioxide">silica</a> nanoparticles, and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_nanotube" title="Carbon nanotube">carbon nanotubes</a> for stain-resistant textiles.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-americanelements&#95;18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-americanelements-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-emergingnano&#95;19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-emergingnano-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Governments moved to promote and <a href="/wiki/Funding_of_science" title="Funding of science">fund research</a> into nanotechnology, such as American the <a href="/wiki/National_Nanotechnology_Initiative" title="National Nanotechnology Initiative">National Nanotechnology Initiative</a>, which formalized a size-based definition of nanotechnology and established research funding, and in Europe via the European <a href="/wiki/Framework_Programmes_for_Research_and_Technological_Development" title="Framework Programmes for Research and Technological Development">Framework Programmes for Research and Technological Development</a>.
</p><p>By the mid-2000s scientific attention began to flourish. Nanotechnology roadmaps centered on atomically precise manipulation of matter and discussed existing and projected capabilities, goals, and applications.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-PNRoadmap&#95;20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PNRoadmap-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-NASAroadmap&#95;21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NASAroadmap-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Fundamental_concepts">Fundamental concepts</h2></div>
<p>Nanotechnology is the science and engineering of functional systems at the molecular scale. In its original sense, nanotechnology refers to the projected ability to construct items from the bottom up making complete, high-performance products.
</p><p>One <a href="/wiki/Nanometer" class="mw-redirect" title="Nanometer">nanometer</a> (nm) is one billionth, or 10<sup>−9</sup>, of a meter. By comparison, typical carbon–carbon <a href="/wiki/Bond_length" title="Bond length">bond lengths</a>, or the spacing between these <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atoms</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Molecule" title="Molecule">molecule</a>, are in the range <span class="nowrap">0.12–0.15 nm</span>, and <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a>'s diameter is around 2&#160;nm. On the other hand, the smallest <a href="/wiki/Cell_(biology)" title="Cell (biology)">cellular</a> life forms, the bacteria of the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Mycoplasma" title="Mycoplasma">Mycoplasma</a></i>, are around 200&#160;nm in length. By convention, nanotechnology is taken as the scale range <span class="nowrap">1 to 100 nm</span>, following the definition used by the American <a href="/wiki/National_Nanotechnology_Initiative" title="National Nanotechnology Initiative">National Nanotechnology Initiative</a>. The lower limit is set by the size of atoms (hydrogen has the smallest atoms, which have an approximately ,25&#160;nm <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_diameter" title="Kinetic diameter">kinetic diameter</a>). The upper limit is more or less arbitrary, but is around the size below which phenomena not observed in larger structures start to become apparent and can be made use of.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These phenomena make nanotechnology distinct from devices that are merely miniaturized versions of an equivalent <a href="/wiki/Macroscopic_scale" title="Macroscopic scale">macroscopic</a> device; such devices are on a larger scale and come under the description of <a href="/wiki/Microtechnology" title="Microtechnology">microtechnology</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>To put that scale in another context, the comparative size of a nanometer to a meter is the same as that of a marble to the size of the earth.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-NationalG&#95;24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NationalG-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Two main approaches are used in nanotechnology. In the "bottom-up" approach, materials and devices are built from molecular components which <a href="/wiki/Self-assembly" title="Self-assembly">assemble themselves</a> chemically by principles of <a href="/wiki/Molecular_recognition" class="mw-redirect" title="Molecular recognition">molecular recognition</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ReferenceA&#95;25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In the "top-down" approach, nano-objects are constructed from larger entities without atomic-level control.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Areas of physics such as <a href="/wiki/Nanoelectronics" title="Nanoelectronics">nanoelectronics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nanomechanics" title="Nanomechanics">nanomechanics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nanophotonics" title="Nanophotonics">nanophotonics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nanoionics" title="Nanoionics">nanoionics</a> have evolved to provide nanotechnology's scientific foundation.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Larger_to_smaller:_a_materials_perspective">Larger to smaller: a materials perspective</h3></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Atomic_resolution_Au100.JPG" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Atomic_resolution_Au100.JPG/250px-Atomic_resolution_Au100.JPG" decoding="async" width="250" height="240" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/ec/Atomic_resolution_Au100.JPG 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="480" /></a><figcaption>Image of <a href="/wiki/Surface_reconstruction" title="Surface reconstruction">reconstruction</a> on a clean <a href="/wiki/Gold" title="Gold">Gold</a>(<a href="/wiki/Miller_index" title="Miller index">100</a>) surface, as visualized using <a href="/wiki/Scanning_tunneling_microscopy" class="mw-redirect" title="Scanning tunneling microscopy">scanning tunneling microscopy</a>. The positions of the individual atoms composing the surface are visible.</figcaption></figure>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nanomaterials" title="Nanomaterials">Nanomaterials</a></div>
<p>Several phenomena become pronounced as system size. These include <a href="/wiki/Statistical_mechanics" title="Statistical mechanics">statistical mechanical</a> effects, as well as <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanical</a> effects, for example, the "<a href="/wiki/Quantum" title="Quantum">quantum</a> size effect" in which the electronic properties of solids alter along with reductions in particle size. Such effects do not apply at macro or micro dimensions. However, quantum effects can become significant when nanometer scales. Additionally, physical (mechanical, electrical, optical, etc.) properties change versus macroscopic systems. One example is the increase in surface area to volume ratio altering mechanical, thermal, and catalytic properties of materials. <a href="/wiki/Diffusion" title="Diffusion">Diffusion</a> and reactions can be different as well. Systems with fast ion transport are referred to as nanoionics. The mechanical properties of nanosystems are of interest in research.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Simple_to_complex:_a_molecular_perspective">Simple to complex: a molecular perspective</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Molecular_self-assembly" title="Molecular self-assembly">Molecular self-assembly</a></div>
<p>Modern <a href="/wiki/Chemical_synthesis" title="Chemical synthesis">synthetic chemistry</a> can prepare small molecules of almost any structure. These methods are used to manufacture a wide variety of useful chemicals such as <a href="/wiki/Drug" title="Drug">pharmaceuticals</a> or commercial <a href="/wiki/Polymer" title="Polymer">polymers</a>. This ability raises the question of extending this kind of control to the next-larger level, seeking methods to assemble single molecules into <a href="/wiki/Supramolecular_assembly" class="mw-redirect" title="Supramolecular assembly">supramolecular assemblies</a> consisting of many molecules arranged in a well-defined manner.
</p><p>These approaches utilize the concepts of molecular <a href="/wiki/Self-assembly" title="Self-assembly">self-assembly</a> and/or <a href="/wiki/Supramolecular_chemistry" title="Supramolecular chemistry">supramolecular chemistry</a> to automatically arrange themselves into a useful conformation through a <a href="/wiki/Top-down_and_bottom-up#Nanotechnology" class="mw-redirect" title="Top-down and bottom-up">bottom-up</a> approach. The concept of <a href="/wiki/Molecular_recognition" class="mw-redirect" title="Molecular recognition">molecular recognition</a> is important: molecules can be designed so that a specific configuration or arrangement is favored due to <a href="/wiki/Noncovalent_bonding" class="mw-redirect" title="Noncovalent bonding">non-covalent</a> <a href="/wiki/Intermolecular_force" title="Intermolecular force">intermolecular forces</a>. The Watson–Crick <a href="/wiki/Base_pair" title="Base pair">basepairing</a> rules are a direct result of this, as is the specificity of an <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzyme</a> targeting a single <a href="/wiki/Substrate_(biochemistry)" class="mw-redirect" title="Substrate (biochemistry)">substrate</a>, or the specific <a href="/wiki/Protein_folding" title="Protein folding">folding of a protein</a>. Thus, components can be designed to be complementary and mutually attractive so that they make a more complex and useful whole.
</p><p>Such bottom-up approaches should be capable of producing devices in parallel and be much cheaper than top-down methods, but could potentially be overwhelmed as the size and complexity of the desired assembly increases. Most useful structures require complex and thermodynamically unlikely arrangements of atoms. Nevertheless, many examples of self-assembly based on molecular recognition in exist in <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biology</a>, most notably Watson–Crick basepairing and enzyme-substrate interactions.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Molecular_nanotechnology:_a_long-term_view">Molecular nanotechnology: a long-term view</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Molecular_nanotechnology" title="Molecular nanotechnology">Molecular nanotechnology</a></div>
<figure typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Protein_translation.gif" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/94/Protein_translation.gif" decoding="async" width="250" height="250" class="mw-file-element" data-file-width="250" data-file-height="250" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Ribosome" title="Ribosome">Ribosome</a> translating <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Biological_machine" class="mw-redirect" title="Biological machine">biological machine</a> functioning as a <a href="/wiki/Molecular_assembler" title="Molecular assembler">molecular assembler</a>. <a href="/wiki/Protein_domain_dynamics" class="mw-redirect" title="Protein domain dynamics">Protein domain dynamics</a> can now be seen by <a href="/wiki/Neutron_spin_echo" title="Neutron spin echo">neutron spin echo</a> spectroscopy.</figcaption></figure> 
<p>Molecular nanotechnology, sometimes called molecular manufacturing, concerns engineered nanosystems (nanoscale machines) operating on the molecular scale. Molecular nanotechnology is especially associated with <a href="/wiki/Molecular_assembler" title="Molecular assembler">molecular assemblers</a>, machines that can produce a desired structure or device atom-by-atom using the principles of <a href="/wiki/Mechanosynthesis" title="Mechanosynthesis">mechanosynthesis</a>. Manufacturing in the context of <a href="/wiki/Productive_nanosystems" title="Productive nanosystems">productive nanosystems</a> is not related to conventional technologies used to manufacture nanomaterials such as carbon nanotubes and nanoparticles.
</p><p>When Drexler independently coined and popularized the term "nanotechnology", he envisioned manufacturing technology based on <a href="/wiki/Molecular_machine" title="Molecular machine">molecular machine</a> systems. The premise was that molecular-scale biological analogies of traditional machine components demonstrated molecular machines were possible: biology was full of examples of sophisticated, <a href="/wiki/Stochastic" title="Stochastic">stochastically</a> optimized <a href="/wiki/Molecular_machine#Biological" title="Molecular machine">biological machines</a>.
</p><p>Drexler and other researchers<sup id="cite&#95;ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> have proposed that advanced nanotechnology ultimately could be based on mechanical engineering principles, namely, a manufacturing technology based on the mechanical functionality of these components (such as gears, bearings, motors, and structural members) that would enable programmable, positional assembly to atomic specification.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The physics and engineering performance of exemplar designs were analyzed in Drexler's book <i>Nanosystems: Molecular Machinery, Manufacturing, and Computation</i>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Nanotsystems&#95;2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nanotsystems-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In general, assembling devices on the atomic scale requires positioning atoms on other atoms of comparable size and stickiness. <a href="/wiki/Carlo_Montemagno" title="Carlo Montemagno">Carlo Montemagno</a>'s view is that future nanosystems will be hybrids of silicon technology and biological molecular machines.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Richard_Smalley" title="Richard Smalley">Richard Smalley</a> argued that mechanosynthesis was impossible due to difficulties in mechanically manipulating individual molecules.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>This led to an exchange of letters in the <a href="/wiki/American_Chemical_Society" title="American Chemical Society">American Chemical Society</a> publication <i><a href="/wiki/Chemical_%26_Engineering_News" title="Chemical &amp; Engineering News">Chemical &amp; Engineering News</a></i> in 2003.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Though biology clearly demonstrates that molecular machines are possible, non-biological molecular machines remained in their infancy. <a href="/wiki/Alex_Zettl" title="Alex Zettl">Alex Zettl</a> and colleagues at Lawrence Berkeley Laboratories and UC Berkeley<sup id="cite&#95;ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> constructed at least three molecular devices whose motion is controlled via changing voltage: a nanotube <a href="/wiki/Nanomotor" title="Nanomotor">nanomotor</a>, a molecular actuator,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and a nanoelectromechanical relaxation oscillator.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Ho and Lee at <a href="/wiki/Cornell_University" title="Cornell University">Cornell University</a> in 1999 used a scanning tunneling microscope to move an individual carbon monoxide molecule (CO) to an individual iron atom (Fe) sitting on a flat silver crystal and chemically bound the CO to the Fe by applying a voltage.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Research">Research</h2></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Rotaxane_cartoon.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Rotaxane_cartoon.jpg/250px-Rotaxane_cartoon.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="168" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/cd/Rotaxane_cartoon.jpg 2x" data-file-width="361" data-file-height="242" /></a><figcaption>Graphical representation of a <a href="/wiki/Rotaxane" title="Rotaxane">rotaxane</a>, useful as a <a href="/wiki/Molecular_switch" title="Molecular switch">molecular switch</a></figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:DNA_tetrahedron_white.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/DNA_tetrahedron_white.png/250px-DNA_tetrahedron_white.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="250" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/DNA_tetrahedron_white.png/500px-DNA_tetrahedron_white.png 2x" data-file-width="850" data-file-height="850" /></a><figcaption>This DNA <a href="/wiki/Tetrahedron" title="Tetrahedron">tetrahedron</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Goodman05&#95;36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goodman05-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> is an artificially <a href="/wiki/Nucleic_acid_design" title="Nucleic acid design">designed</a> nanostructure of the type made in the field of <a href="/wiki/DNA_nanotechnology" title="DNA nanotechnology">DNA nanotechnology</a>. Each edge of the tetrahedron is a 20 base pair DNA <a href="/wiki/Nucleic_acid_double_helix" title="Nucleic acid double helix">double helix</a>, and each vertex is a three-arm junction.</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:C60_Buckyball.gif" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/C60_Buckyball.gif/250px-C60_Buckyball.gif" decoding="async" width="230" height="222" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/32/C60_Buckyball.gif 2x" data-file-width="438" data-file-height="422" /></a><figcaption>Rotating view of C<sub>60</sub>, one kind of fullerene</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Achermann7RED.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Achermann7RED.jpg/250px-Achermann7RED.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="354" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Achermann7RED.jpg/500px-Achermann7RED.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1500" data-file-height="2122" /></a><figcaption>This device transfers energy from nano-thin layers of <a href="/wiki/Quantum_well" title="Quantum well">quantum wells</a> to nanocrystals above them, causing the nanocrystals to emit visible light.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></figcaption></figure>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Nanomaterials">Nanomaterials</h3></div>
<p>Many areas of science develop or study materials having unique properties arising from their nanoscale dimensions.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Interface_and_colloid_science" title="Interface and colloid science">Interface and colloid science</a> produced many materials that may be useful in nanotechnology, such as carbon nanotubes and other <a href="/wiki/Fullerenes" class="mw-redirect" title="Fullerenes">fullerenes</a>, and various nanoparticles and <a href="/wiki/Nanorod" title="Nanorod">nanorods</a>. Nanomaterials with fast ion transport are related to nanoionics and nanoelectronics.</li>
<li>Nanoscale materials can be used for bulk applications; most commercial applications of nanotechnology are of this flavor.</li>
<li>Progress has been made in using these materials for <a href="/wiki/Nanomedicine" title="Nanomedicine">medical applications</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Tissue_engineering" title="Tissue engineering">tissue engineering</a>, <a href="/wiki/Drug_delivery" title="Drug delivery">drug delivery</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antibacterials" class="mw-redirect" title="Antibacterials">antibacterials</a> and <a href="/wiki/Biosensor" title="Biosensor">biosensors</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li>Nanoscale materials such as <a href="/wiki/Nanopillar" title="Nanopillar">nanopillars</a> are used in <a href="/wiki/Solar_cell" title="Solar cell">solar cells</a>.</li>
<li>Applications incorporating semiconductor <a href="/wiki/Nanoparticle" title="Nanoparticle">nanoparticles</a> in products such as display technology, lighting, solar cells and biological imaging; see <a href="/wiki/Quantum_dot" title="Quantum dot">quantum dots</a>.</li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Bottom-up_approaches">Bottom-up approaches</h3></div>
<p>The bottom-up approach seeks to arrange smaller components into more complex assemblies.
</p>
<ul><li>DNA nanotechnology utilizes Watson–Crick basepairing to construct well-defined structures out of DNA and other <a href="/wiki/Nucleic_acid" title="Nucleic acid">nucleic acids</a>.</li>
<li>Approaches from the field of "classical" chemical synthesis (inorganic and <a href="/wiki/Organic_synthesis" title="Organic synthesis">organic synthesis</a>) aim at designing molecules with well-defined shape (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Bis-peptide" class="mw-redirect" title="Bis-peptide">bis-peptides</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Levins&#95;44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Levins-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>).</li>
<li>More generally, molecular self-assembly seeks to use concepts of supramolecular chemistry, and molecular recognition in particular, to cause single-molecule components to automatically arrange themselves into some useful conformation.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Atomic_force_microscopy" title="Atomic force microscopy">Atomic force microscope</a> tips can be used as a nanoscale "write head" to deposit a chemical upon a surface in a desired pattern in a process called <a href="/wiki/Dip-pen_nanolithography" title="Dip-pen nanolithography">dip-pen nanolithography</a>. This technique fits into the larger subfield of <a href="/wiki/Nanolithography" title="Nanolithography">nanolithography</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Molecular-beam_epitaxy" title="Molecular-beam epitaxy">Molecular-beam epitaxy</a> allows for bottom-up assemblies of materials, most notably semiconductor materials commonly used in chip and computing applications, stacks, gating, and <a href="/wiki/Nanowire_lasers" title="Nanowire lasers">nanowire lasers</a>.</li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Top-down_approaches">Top-down approaches</h3></div>
<p>These seek to create smaller devices by using larger ones to direct their assembly.
</p>
<ul><li>Many technologies that descended from conventional <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor_fabrication" class="mw-redirect" title="Semiconductor fabrication">solid-state silicon methods</a> for fabricating <a href="/wiki/Microprocessor" title="Microprocessor">microprocessors</a> are capable of creating features smaller than 100&#160;nm. <a href="/wiki/Giant_magnetoresistance" title="Giant magnetoresistance">Giant magnetoresistance</a>-based hard drives already on the market fit this description,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> as do <a href="/wiki/Atomic_layer_deposition" title="Atomic layer deposition">atomic layer deposition</a> (ALD) techniques. <a href="/wiki/Peter_Gr%C3%BCnberg" title="Peter Grünberg">Peter Grünberg</a> and <a href="/wiki/Albert_Fert" title="Albert Fert">Albert Fert</a> received the Nobel Prize in Physics in 2007 for their discovery of giant magnetoresistance and contributions to the field of <a href="/wiki/Spintronics" title="Spintronics">spintronics</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li>Solid-state techniques can be used to create <a href="/wiki/Nanoelectromechanical_systems" title="Nanoelectromechanical systems">nanoelectromechanical systems</a> or NEMS, which are related to <a href="/wiki/Microelectromechanical_systems" class="mw-redirect" title="Microelectromechanical systems">microelectromechanical systems</a> or MEMS.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Focused_ion_beam" title="Focused ion beam">Focused ion beams</a> can directly remove material, or even deposit material when suitable precursor gasses are applied at the same time. For example, this technique is used routinely to create sub-100&#160;nm sections of material for analysis in <a href="/wiki/Transmission_electron_microscopy" title="Transmission electron microscopy">transmission electron microscopy</a>.</li>
<li>Atomic force microscope tips can be used as a nanoscale "write head" to deposit a resist, which is then followed by an etching process to remove material in a top-down method.</li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Functional_approaches">Functional approaches</h3></div>
<p>Functional approaches seek to develop useful components without regard to how they might be assembled.
</p>
<ul><li>Magnetic assembly for the synthesis of <a href="/wiki/Anisotropy" title="Anisotropy">anisotropic</a> <a href="/wiki/Superparamagnetic" class="mw-redirect" title="Superparamagnetic">superparamagnetic</a> materials such as magnetic nano chains.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ReferenceA&#95;25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Molecular_scale_electronics" class="mw-redirect" title="Molecular scale electronics">Molecular scale electronics</a> seeks to develop molecules with useful electronic properties. These could be used as single-molecule components in a nanoelectronic device,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> such as <a href="/wiki/Rotaxane" title="Rotaxane">rotaxane</a>.</li>
<li>Synthetic chemical methods can be used to create <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_molecular_motor" title="Synthetic molecular motor">synthetic molecular motors</a>, such as in a so-called <a href="/wiki/Nanocar" title="Nanocar">nanocar</a>.</li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Biomimetic_approaches">Biomimetic approaches</h3></div>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Bionics" title="Bionics">Bionics</a> or <a href="/wiki/Biomimicry" class="mw-redirect" title="Biomimicry">biomimicry</a> seeks to apply biological methods and systems found in nature to the study and design of engineering systems and modern technology. <a href="/wiki/Biomineralization" title="Biomineralization">Biomineralization</a> is one example of the systems studied.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bionanotechnology" class="mw-redirect" title="Bionanotechnology">Bionanotechnology</a> is the use of <a href="/wiki/Biomolecule" title="Biomolecule">biomolecules</a> for applications in nanotechnology, including the use of viruses and lipid assemblies.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nanocellulose" title="Nanocellulose">Nanocellulose</a>, a nanopolymer often used for bulk-scale applications, has gained interest owing to its useful properties such as abundance, high aspect ratio, good <a href="/wiki/Mechanical_properties" class="mw-redirect" title="Mechanical properties">mechanical properties</a>, <a href="/wiki/Renewable_resource" title="Renewable resource">renewability</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Biocompatibility" title="Biocompatibility">biocompatibility</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Speculative">Speculative</h3></div>
<p>These subfields seek to <a href="/wiki/Futures_studies" title="Futures studies">anticipate</a> what inventions nanotechnology might yield, or attempt to propose an agenda along which inquiry could progress. These often take a big-picture view, with more emphasis on societal implications than engineering details.
</p>
<ul><li>Molecular nanotechnology is a proposed approach that involves manipulating single molecules in finely controlled, deterministic ways. This is more theoretical than the other subfields, and many of its proposed techniques are beyond current capabilities.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Nanorobotics" title="Nanorobotics">Nanorobotics</a> considers self-sufficient machines operating at the nanoscale. There are hopes for applying nanorobots in medicine.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Nevertheless, progress on innovative materials and patented methodologies has been demonstrated.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li>Productive nanosystems are "systems of nanosystems" could produce atomically precise parts for other nanosystems, not necessarily using novel nanoscale-emergent properties, but well-understood fundamentals of manufacturing. Because of the discrete (i.e. atomic) nature of matter and the possibility of exponential growth, this stage could form the basis of another industrial revolution. <a href="/wiki/Mihail_Roco" title="Mihail Roco">Mihail Roco</a> proposed four states of nanotechnology that seem to parallel the technical progress of the Industrial Revolution, progressing from passive nanostructures to active nanodevices to complex <a href="/wiki/Nanomachine" class="mw-redirect" title="Nanomachine">nanomachines</a> and ultimately to productive nanosystems.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>55<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Programmable_matter" title="Programmable matter">Programmable matter</a> seeks to design materials whose properties can be easily, reversibly and externally controlled though a fusion of <a href="/wiki/Information_science" title="Information science">information science</a> and <a href="/wiki/Materials_science" title="Materials science">materials science</a>.</li>
<li>Due to the popularity and media exposure of the term nanotechnology, the words <a href="/wiki/Picotechnology" title="Picotechnology">picotechnology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Femtotechnology" title="Femtotechnology">femtotechnology</a> have been coined in analogy to it, although these are used only informally.</li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Dimensionality_in_nanomaterials">Dimensionality in nanomaterials</h3></div>
<p>Nanomaterials can be classified in 0D, 1D, 2D and 3D <a href="/wiki/Nanomaterials" title="Nanomaterials">nanomaterials</a>. Dimensionality plays a major role in determining the characteristic of nanomaterials including <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/physical" class="extiw" title="wikt:physical">physical</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chemical" class="mw-redirect" title="Chemical">chemical</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Biological" class="mw-redirect" title="Biological">biological</a> characteristics. With the decrease in dimensionality, an increase in surface-to-volume ratio is observed. This indicates that smaller dimensional <a href="/wiki/Nanomaterials" title="Nanomaterials">nanomaterials</a> have higher surface area compared to 3D nanomaterials. <a href="/wiki/Two_dimensional_(2D)_nanomaterials" class="mw-redirect" title="Two dimensional (2D) nanomaterials">Two dimensional (2D) nanomaterials</a> have been extensively investigated for <a href="/wiki/Electronics" title="Electronics">electronic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biomedical" class="mw-redirect" title="Biomedical">biomedical</a>, <a href="/wiki/Drug_delivery" title="Drug delivery">drug delivery</a> and <a href="/wiki/Biosensor" title="Biosensor">biosensor</a> applications.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Tools_and_techniques">Tools and techniques</h2></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:AFMsetup.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/AFMsetup.jpg/500px-AFMsetup.jpg" decoding="async" width="340" height="268" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5e/AFMsetup.jpg 2x" data-file-width="721" data-file-height="569" /></a><figcaption>Typical <a href="/wiki/Atomic_force_microscopy" title="Atomic force microscopy">AFM</a> setup. A microfabricated <a href="/wiki/Cantilever" title="Cantilever">cantilever</a> with a sharp tip is deflected by features on a sample surface, much like in a <a href="/wiki/Phonograph" title="Phonograph">phonograph</a> but on a much smaller scale. A <a href="/wiki/Laser" title="Laser">laser</a> beam reflects off the backside of the cantilever into a set of <a href="/wiki/Photodetector" title="Photodetector">photodetectors</a>, allowing the deflection to be measured and assembled into an image of the surface.</figcaption></figure>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Scanning_microscopes">Scanning microscopes</h3></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Atomic_force_microscopy" title="Atomic force microscopy">atomic force microscope</a> (AFM) and the <a href="/wiki/Scanning_tunneling_microscope" title="Scanning tunneling microscope">scanning tunneling microscope</a> (STM) are two versions of scanning probes that are used for nano-scale observation. Other types of <a href="/wiki/Scanning_probe_microscopy" title="Scanning probe microscopy">scanning probe microscopy</a> have much higher resolution, since they are not limited by the wavelengths of sound or light.
</p><p>The tip of a scanning probe can also be used to manipulate nanostructures (positional assembly). <a href="/wiki/Feature-oriented_scanning" title="Feature-oriented scanning">Feature-oriented scanning</a> may be a promising way to implement these nano-scale manipulations via an automatic <a href="/wiki/Algorithm" title="Algorithm">algorithm</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-feature2004&#95;56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-feature2004-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-fospm2011&#95;57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fospm2011-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>57<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, this is still a slow process because of low velocity of the microscope.
</p><p>The top-down approach anticipates nanodevices that must be built piece by piece in stages, much as manufactured items are made. <a href="/wiki/Scanning_probe_microscopy" title="Scanning probe microscopy">Scanning probe microscopy</a> is an important technique both for characterization and synthesis. Atomic force microscopes and scanning tunneling microscopes can be used to look at surfaces and to move atoms around. By designing different tips for these microscopes, they can be used for carving out structures on surfaces and to help guide self-assembling structures. By using, for example, feature-oriented scanning approach, atoms or molecules can be moved around on a surface with scanning probe microscopy techniques.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-feature2004&#95;56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-feature2004-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-fospm2011&#95;57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fospm2011-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>57<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Lithography">Lithography</h3></div>
<p>Various techniques of lithography, such as <a href="/wiki/Optical_lithography" class="mw-redirect" title="Optical lithography">optical lithography</a>, <a href="/wiki/X-ray_lithography" title="X-ray lithography">X-ray lithography</a>, dip pen lithography, <a href="/wiki/Electron_beam_lithography" class="mw-redirect" title="Electron beam lithography">electron beam lithography</a> or <a href="/wiki/Nanoimprint_lithography" title="Nanoimprint lithography">nanoimprint lithography</a> offer top-down fabrication techniques where a bulk material is reduced to a nano-scale pattern.
</p><p>Another group of nano-technological techniques include those used for fabrication of <a href="/wiki/Ion_track_technology_(track_etching)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ion track technology (track etching)">nanotubes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ion_track_technology_(track_replication)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ion track technology (track replication)">nanowires</a>, those used in semiconductor fabrication such as deep ultraviolet lithography, electron beam lithography, focused ion beam machining, nanoimprint lithography, <a href="/wiki/Atomic_layer_deposition" title="Atomic layer deposition">atomic layer deposition</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Molecular_vapor_deposition" title="Molecular vapor deposition">molecular vapor deposition</a>, and further including molecular self-assembly techniques such as those employing di-block <a href="/wiki/Copolymer" title="Copolymer">copolymers</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid26295171&#95;58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid26295171-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Bottom-up">Bottom-up</h4></div>
<p>In contrast, bottom-up techniques build or grow larger structures atom by atom or molecule by molecule. These techniques include chemical synthesis, <a href="/wiki/Self-assembly" title="Self-assembly">self-assembly</a> and positional assembly. <a href="/wiki/Dual-polarization_interferometry" title="Dual-polarization interferometry">Dual-polarization interferometry</a> is one tool suitable for characterization of self-assembled thin films. Another variation of the bottom-up approach is <a href="/wiki/Molecular-beam_epitaxy" title="Molecular-beam epitaxy">molecular-beam epitaxy</a> or MBE. Researchers at <a href="/wiki/Bell_Telephone_Laboratories" class="mw-redirect" title="Bell Telephone Laboratories">Bell Telephone Laboratories</a> including <a href="/wiki/John_R._Arthur_Jr." title="John R. Arthur Jr.">John R. Arthur</a>. <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Y._Cho" title="Alfred Y. Cho">Alfred Y. Cho</a>, and Art C. Gossard developed and implemented MBE as a research tool in the late 1960s and 1970s. Samples made by MBE were key to the discovery of the <a href="/wiki/Fractional_quantum_Hall_effect" title="Fractional quantum Hall effect">fractional quantum Hall effect</a> for which the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Nobel_laureates_in_Physics" title="List of Nobel laureates in Physics">1998 Nobel Prize in Physics</a> was awarded. MBE lays down atomically precise layers of atoms and, in the process, build up complex structures. Important for research on semiconductors, MBE is also widely used to make samples and devices for the newly emerging field of <a href="/wiki/Spintronics" title="Spintronics">spintronics</a>.
</p><p>Therapeutic products based on responsive <a href="/wiki/Nanomaterials" title="Nanomaterials">nanomaterials</a>, such as the highly deformable, stress-sensitive <a href="/wiki/Transfersome" title="Transfersome">transfersome</a> vesicles, are approved for human use in some countries.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Applications">Applications</h2></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Threshold_formation_nowatermark.gif" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Threshold_formation_nowatermark.gif/500px-Threshold_formation_nowatermark.gif" decoding="async" width="450" height="204" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/43/Threshold_formation_nowatermark.gif 2x" data-file-width="722" data-file-height="328" /></a><figcaption>One of the major applications of nanotechnology is in the area of <a href="/wiki/Nanoelectronics" title="Nanoelectronics">nanoelectronics</a> with <a href="/wiki/MOSFET" title="MOSFET">MOSFETs</a> being made of small <a href="/wiki/Nanowire" title="Nanowire">nanowires</a> ≈10 nm in length. Here is a simulation of such a nanowire.</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><span><video id="mwe_player_0" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/A-simple-and-fast-fabrication-of-a-both-self-cleanable-and-deep-UV-antireflective-quartz-1556-276X-7-430-S1.ogv/250px--A-simple-and-fast-fabrication-of-a-both-self-cleanable-and-deep-UV-antireflective-quartz-1556-276X-7-430-S1.ogv.jpg" controls="" preload="none" data-mw-tmh="" class="mw-file-element" width="250" height="188" data-durationhint="34" data-mwtitle="A-simple-and-fast-fabrication-of-a-both-self-cleanable-and-deep-UV-antireflective-quartz-1556-276X-7-430-S1.ogv" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" resource="/wiki/File:A-simple-and-fast-fabrication-of-a-both-self-cleanable-and-deep-UV-antireflective-quartz-1556-276X-7-430-S1.ogv"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/6/6d/A-simple-and-fast-fabrication-of-a-both-self-cleanable-and-deep-UV-antireflective-quartz-1556-276X-7-430-S1.ogv/A-simple-and-fast-fabrication-of-a-both-self-cleanable-and-deep-UV-antireflective-quartz-1556-276X-7-430-S1.ogv.240p.vp9.webm" type="video/webm; <figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Nanowire_laser.png" class="mw-file-description"><img
